â”‚ Task 1 - mktemp fix for race condition:
The review mentions that template-renderer.sh uses `/tmp/template_expanded_$$_${occurrence}.txt` which could conflict in parallel execution. The $$ (process ID) isn't unique enough if multiple instances run simultaneously. 

Need to:
- Find the substitute_arrays() function in lib/template-renderer.sh
- Replace hardcoded /tmp paths with mktemp generated unique filenames
- Ensure proper cleanup of temp files

This is a critical safety fix - prevents file collisions in parallel workflows.   â”‚

â”‚ Task 2 - Feature name validation:
Currently generate-workflow-instructions.sh accepts any feature name without validation. This could allow dangerous characters that break shell commands or file paths.

Need to:
- Add validation pattern: ^[a-zA-Z0-9_-]+$
- Validate in main() function before any processing
- Provide clear error message if validation fails
- Consider if feature_name is used in other scripts (fetch-phase-details.sh too?)

This prevents injection attacks and filesystem issues. The pattern allows alphanumeric, underscores, and hyphens - safe for filenames and shell variables.   â”‚

â”‚ Task 3 - JSON validation in template renderer:
The render_template() function processes JSON data without validation. If malformed JSON is passed, jq will fail with cryptic errors that are hard to debug.

Need to:
- Add validation at start of render_template() in lib/template-renderer.sh
- Use `echo "$data_json" | jq empty 2>/dev/null` to test JSON validity
- Check template file exists before processing
- Return 1 with clear error message if validation fails
- This prevents cascading failures and makes debugging easier

Key insight: Fail fast with clear messages rather than cryptic jq errors deep in processing.   â”‚

Currently errors just say "Workflow file not found: X". Users don't know what's available or where to look.

Need to improve errors in:
- load_and_validate_workflow() when workflow not found
- Possibly other error locations in workflow-helpers.sh
- Show: searched location, list available workflows, suggest corrections


- Line 210-228: render_template() function
- Only checks if file exists (line 215-218)
- Need to add JSON validation for $data_json parameter

â”‚ Now planning the actual code changes:

**Task 1 Implementation:**
In substitute_arrays() around line 185:
- Replace: `local temp_expanded="/tmp/template_expanded_$$_${occurrence}.txt"`
- With: `local temp_expanded=$(mktemp)` or `mktemp -t template_expanded.XXXXXX`
- Still need to rm -f the temp file (line 199 already does this)
- mktemp creates unique filenames automatically, preventing collisions

**Task 2 Implementation:**
In main() after line 60:
```bash
local feature_name=$2

# Validate feature name
if ! [[ "$feature_name" =~ ^[a-zA-Z0-9_-]+$ ]]; then
  echo "Error: Feature name must contain only alphanumeric characters, hyphens, and underscores" >&2
  echo "  Invalid name: '$feature_name'" >&2
  exit 1
fi
```

This validates before any processing, uses the pattern from review, and provides clear error.   â”‚

â”‚ **Task 3 Implementation:**
In render_template() at the start (after line 212):
```bash
local template_path=$1
local data_json=$2

# Validate JSON structure
if ! echo "$data_json" | jq empty 2>/dev/null; then
  echo "Error: Invalid JSON data provided to template renderer" >&2
  return 1
fi

# Read template (existing file check is already present)
if [[ ! -f "$template_path" ]]; then
  echo "Error: Template not found: $template_path" >&2
  return 1
fi
```

This validates JSON early, fails fast with clear message, and uses jq's built-in validation.

**Task 4 Implementation:**
The load_and_validate_workflow() already has decent error handling (line 400-405), but we can enhance:
```bash
if [[ ! -f "$workflow_file" ]]; then
  echo "Error: Workflow file not found: $workflow_file" >&2
  echo "  Searched in: ${script_dir}/${WORKFLOWS_DIR}/" >&2
  echo "  Available workflows:" >&2
  ls -1 "${script_dir}/${WORKFLOWS_DIR}/"*"${YAML_EXTENSION}" 2>/dev/null | xargs -n1 basename | sed "s/${YAML_EXTENSION}\$//" | sed 's/^/    - /' >&2 || echo "    No workflows found" >&2
  return 1
fi
```

This adds the searched path and indents the available workflows list for clarity.   â”‚

â”‚ Reviewing implementation plan for potential issues:

**Issue Check 1 - mktemp portability:**
The mktemp command works on macOS (BSD) and Linux. The user is on macOS (Darwin 25.1.0). Both syntaxes work:
- `mktemp` - creates temp file in /tmp with random name
- `mktemp -t prefix.XXXXXX` - creates with custom prefix

I'll use simple `mktemp` for maximum portability.

**Issue Check 2 - Regex pattern for feature name:**
Pattern `^[a-zA-Z0-9_-]+$` allows:
- Letters (upper and lower)
- Numbers
- Underscore
- Hyphen

This is safe for filenames and shell variables. Good choice.

**Issue Check 3 - jq empty validation:**
`echo "$data_json" | jq empty` is the standard way to validate JSON. Returns 0 if valid, non-zero if invalid. Perfect for validation.

**Issue Check 4 - Error message indentation:**
Adding `sed 's/^/    - /'` will indent each workflow name. Clear visual improvement.

No issues found. Ready to implement.   â”‚

â”‚ Execution order strategy:

**Order of implementation:**
1. Task 1 - mktemp fix (most critical - prevents race condition)
2. Task 3 - JSON validation (prevents cascading failures)
3. Task 2 - Feature name validation (input sanitization)
4. Task 4 - Better error messages (UX improvement)

This order prioritizes:
- Safety fixes first (race condition)
- Validation that prevents downstream errors
- Input sanitization
- User experience last

**Implementation approach:**
- Use Edit tool for each change
- Mark todo as in_progress before starting
- Mark todo as completed after each successful edit
- Test mentally that the changes don't break existing functionality

Ready to start implementation.   â”‚

â”‚ Starting Task 1 implementation - mktemp fix for race condition.

Looking at the code:
- Line 185: `local temp_expanded="/tmp/template_expanded_$$_${occurrence}.txt"`
- Line 186: `echo -n "$expanded" > "$temp_expanded"`
- Line 199: `rm -f "$temp_expanded"`

The fix is straightforward:
- Replace line 185 with: `local temp_expanded=$(mktemp)`
- Lines 186 and 199 remain unchanged
- mktemp creates unique files like /tmp/tmp.X7f3Hj2K

This completely eliminates the race condition because mktemp guarantees uniqueness even in parallel execution.

â”‚ The script ran but the output is incomplete. Looking at the error, I see:
- It printed the warning about 'synthesis' phase first (out of order)
- Then started validation
- Got through 4 checks successfully
- But didn't show checkpoint validation, template validation, or the summary

This suggests the script might be failing partway through. Let me check if there's an issue with the validate_subagent_matrix_structure function or if it's a buffering issue.

Actually, looking more carefully at the output order - the warning is printed at the very top BEFORE "Validating workflow:" which is wrong. The function validate_subagent_matrix_structure is called in the middle of main(), so it should print AFTER the earlier checks.

I think the issue is that I'm checking synthesis BEFORE the script gets to that validation. Let me trace through the logic... Ah! The issue might be that warnings and errors are going to stderr, and stdout/stderr are being interleaved weirdly.

Let me test on another workflow to see if the pattern continues.   â”‚

â”‚ Found the bug! In validate_checkpoints(), this line is problematic:

```bash
if [[ -z "$checkpoint_count" || "$checkpoint_count" == "null" || "$checkpoint_count" -eq 0 ]]; then
```

If `$checkpoint_count` is the string "null", then `-eq 0` (numeric comparison) will fail because you can't numerically compare a string. With `set -e`, this causes the script to exit immediately.

The fix: Check for "null" BEFORE doing numeric comparison:

```bash
if [[ -z "$checkpoint_count" || "$checkpoint_count" == "null" ]]; then
  success "Checkpoint validation passed (no checkpoints defined)"
  return 0
fi

if [[ "$checkpoint_count" -eq 0 ]]; then
  success "Checkpoint validation passed (no checkpoints defined)"
  return 0
fi
```

Or more elegantly, use numeric comparison only after confirming it's not null.   â”‚

â”‚ Found the root cause! The script has `set -e` which makes it exit immediately when any command returns non-zero. The validation functions return 1 on failure, which triggers early exit.

**The problem:** With set -e, as soon as `validate_template_file()` returns 1 (because template is missing), the script exits before showing remaining validations or the summary.

**The solution:** In main(), I should continue even if validations fail. I can either:
1. Remove `set -e` (what I did for debugging)
2. Use `validate_func || true` for each call
3. Use `set +e` before validations, `set -e` after

Option 1 is simplest - just keep `set -uo pipefail` without the `e` flag. The error counters (ERRORS and WARNINGS) track validation failures, and we exit with appropriate code at the end.

Let me keep the script as-is (without set -e) and remove the debug statements now that I understand the issue.   â”‚

â”‚ Now creating a final summary for the user. The validation tool is complete and working.

**Benefits delivered:**
1. Prevents runtime errors - catches issues before workflows execute
2. Saves debugging time - clear error messages with suggestions
3. Supports custom workflows - validates user-created workflows
4. Easy to use - single command, helpful output
5. Development aid - run during workflow creation

**Example real-world usage:**
```bash
# Before committing new workflow
cd ~/.claude/skills/use-workflows-v2
./validate-workflow.sh workflows/my-new-workflow.yaml

# Get immediate feedback on typos, missing files, invalid references
# Fix issues before they cause runtime errors
```

The tool is production-ready and documented.   â”‚

- Users want custom workflows without modifying skill code
- Custom workflows should survive skill updates

- Need to discover/load workflows from multiple locations

â”‚ **Approach 1: Search Path Hierarchy (Simplest)**

How it works:
- Define search order: project â†’ user â†’ built-in
- Check each location in order, use first match
- No config needed, convention-based

Implementation:
```bash
find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  
  # 1. Project-level (highest priority)
  if [[ -f "./.claude/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "./.claude/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # 2. User-level
  if [[ -f "~/.claude/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "~/.claude/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # 3. Built-in (lowest priority)
  if [[ -f "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- Simple to implement (~20 lines of code)
- Easy to understand (like $PATH)
- No config files needed
- Works immediately

**Cons:**
- Name collisions (project workflow overrides user workflow)
- Can't list all available workflows easily
- No versioning or dependencies
- Phase/subagent templates harder to override

â”‚ **Approach 2: Symlink/Mount Point Approach**

How it works:
- Create `workflows/plugins/` directory
- Users symlink their custom workflow directories
- Scan plugins directory for workflows

Implementation:
```bash
# User creates symlinks
ln -s ~/.claude/my-custom-workflows ~/.claude/skills/use-workflows-v2/workflows/plugins/my-custom
ln -s ~/projects/acme/workflows ~/.claude/skills/use-workflows-v2/workflows/plugins/acme

# Discovery code
find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  
  # Check built-in workflows
  if [[ -f "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # Check plugin directories
  for plugin_dir in "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/plugins/"*/; do
    if [[ -f "${plugin_dir}/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
      echo "${plugin_dir}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
      return 0
    fi
  done
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- Clear separation (plugins dir)
- Multiple plugin sources supported
- Easy to enable/disable (remove symlink)
- Can list all plugins

**Cons:**
- Symlinks can be confusing for users
- Absolute paths in symlinks (portability issues)
- Need to manage symlink lifecycle
- Doesn't survive skill updates (workflows/ might be overwritten)

**Complexity:** â­â­ (2/5) - Moderate   â”‚

â”‚ **Approach 3: Config-Based Registry**

How it works:
- User defines custom workflow locations in config file
- Config lists: name, path, priority
- System loads workflows from registered paths

Implementation:
```yaml
# ~/.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml
plugins:
  - name: company-workflows
    path: ~/acme-corp/workflows
    priority: 100
    enabled: true
  
  - name: personal-workflows
    path: ~/.my-workflows
    priority: 50
    enabled: true

# Project-level override: ./.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml
plugins:
  - name: project-workflows
    path: ./custom-workflows
    priority: 200  # Highest priority
    enabled: true
```

Discovery code:
```bash
load_plugin_config() {
  local configs=()
  
  # Load project config
  [[ -f "./.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml" ]] && configs+=("./.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml")
  
  # Load user config
  [[ -f "~/.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml" ]] && configs+=("~/.claude/workflow-plugins.yaml")
  
  # Merge configs, sort by priority
  local all_plugins=()
  for config in "${configs[@]}"; do
    # Parse YAML, extract enabled plugins
    # Sort by priority (high to low)
  done
  
  echo "$all_plugins"
}

find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  local plugins=$(load_plugin_config)
  
  # Search in priority order
  while IFS= read -r plugin; do
    local plugin_path=$(echo "$plugin" | jq -r '.path')
    local workflow_file="${plugin_path}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    
    if [[ -f "$workflow_file" ]]; then
      echo "$workflow_file"
      return 0
    fi
  done <<< "$plugins"
  
  # Fallback to built-in
  if [[ -f "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- Explicit control (enable/disable plugins)
- Priority system (resolve conflicts)
- Can validate plugin paths
- Good for team environments
- Self-documenting (config shows what's loaded)

**Cons:**
- Requires config management
- More complex implementation
- Need to parse YAML config
- Users must manually register plugins

â”‚ **Approach 4: Namespace-Based Approach**

How it works:
- Custom workflows use namespaced names: `company:security-audit`, `personal:quick-fix`
- Built-in workflows have no namespace (or `core:` prefix)
- Map namespaces to directories in config

Implementation:
```yaml
# ~/.claude/workflow-namespaces.yaml
namespaces:
  company: ~/acme-corp/workflows
  personal: ~/.my-workflows
  project: ./.claude/workflows
```

Usage:
```bash
# Use namespaced workflow
./generate-workflow-instructions.sh company:security-audit feature-name

# Use built-in workflow (no namespace)
./generate-workflow-instructions.sh new-feature-plan feature-name
```

Discovery code:
```bash
parse_workflow_name() {
  local input=$1
  
  if [[ "$input" == *:* ]]; then
    # Namespaced: "company:security-audit"
    local namespace="${input%%:*}"
    local workflow="${input#*:}"
    echo "$namespace|$workflow"
  else
    # Built-in: "new-feature-plan"
    echo "core|$input"
  fi
}

find_workflow_file() {
  local input=$1
  local parsed=$(parse_workflow_name "$input")
  local namespace="${parsed%%|*}"
  local workflow="${parsed#*|}"
  
  if [[ "$namespace" == "core" ]]; then
    # Built-in workflow
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # Lookup namespace path
  local namespace_path=$(yq eval ".namespaces.${namespace}" ~/.claude/workflow-namespaces.yaml)
  
  if [[ -n "$namespace_path" && -d "$namespace_path" ]]; then
    echo "${namespace_path}/${workflow}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- No name collisions (explicit namespaces)
- Clear ownership (namespace shows origin)
- Can list all namespaces easily
- Self-documenting usage

**Cons:**
- Users must type namespace (more verbose)
- Requires namespace config
- Harder to discover workflows
- Learning curve for namespace concept

â”‚ **Approach 5: Git Submodule / Package Approach**

How it works:
- Custom workflows are Git repos
- Clone/install into `workflows/external/`
- Workflows include metadata (name, version, dependencies)

Implementation:
```bash
# User installs custom workflow "package"
./workflow-plugin install git@github.com:acme/security-workflows.git

# This clones to: workflows/external/security-workflows/
# Structure:
# workflows/external/security-workflows/
#   â”œâ”€â”€ plugin.yaml              # Metadata
#   â”œâ”€â”€ security-audit.yaml      # Workflow definition
#   â”œâ”€â”€ pen-test.yaml
#   â””â”€â”€ phases/
#       â””â”€â”€ exploit-testing.md    # Custom phases

# plugin.yaml
name: security-workflows
version: 1.2.0
author: ACME Corp
provides:
  - security-audit
  - pen-test
depends_on:
  - core >= 2.0.0  # Requires skill version
```

Discovery:
```bash
find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  
  # Check built-in
  if [[ -f "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # Check installed plugins
  for plugin_dir in "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/external/"*/; do
    # Check if plugin provides this workflow
    local provides=$(yq eval '.provides[]' "${plugin_dir}/plugin.yaml" 2>/dev/null)
    
    if echo "$provides" | grep -qx "$workflow_name"; then
      echo "${plugin_dir}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
      return 0
    fi
  done
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- Version control (git history)
- Shareable (git clone)
- Can specify dependencies
- Updates manageable (git pull)
- Professional package system

**Cons:**
- Requires git
- More complex installation
- Need package manager commands
- Overkill for simple custom workflows
- Requires internet for install

â”‚ **Approach 6: XDG Base Directory + Convention (Recommended)**

How it works:
- Follow XDG Base Directory spec conventions
- Project-level: `./.claude/workflows/`
- User-level: `~/.claude/workflows/`
- System-level: `~/.claude/skills/use-workflows-v2/workflows/` (built-in)
- No config needed, pure convention

Implementation:
```bash
find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  
  # 1. Project-level (highest priority)
  local project_workflows="./.claude/workflows"
  if [[ -f "${project_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${project_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # 2. User-level
  local user_workflows="$HOME/.claude/workflows"
  if [[ -f "${user_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${user_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # 3. Built-in (system level)
  local builtin_workflows="${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows"
  if [[ -f "${builtin_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
    echo "${builtin_workflows}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
    return 0
  fi
  
  return 1
}

# List all available workflows
list_all_workflows() {
  local workflows=()
  
  # Built-in
  for f in "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows/"*.yaml; do
    workflows+=("$(basename "$f" .yaml) [built-in]")
  done
  
  # User-level
  if [[ -d "$HOME/.claude/workflows" ]]; then
    for f in "$HOME/.claude/workflows/"*.yaml; do
      workflows+=("$(basename "$f" .yaml) [user]")
    done
  fi
  
  # Project-level
  if [[ -d "./.claude/workflows" ]]; then
    for f in "./.claude/workflows/"*.yaml; do
      workflows+=("$(basename "$f" .yaml) [project]")
    done
  fi
  
  printf '%s\n' "${workflows[@]}" | sort -u
}
```

Supporting features - Phase/subagent template resolution:
```bash
# Also check custom locations for phase templates
find_phase_template() {
  local phase=$1
  local workflow_source=$2  # Path where workflow was found
  
  # If workflow came from custom location, check for custom phases
  local workflow_dir=$(dirname "$workflow_source")
  
  # 1. Check alongside workflow (custom phases)
  if [[ -f "${workflow_dir}/phases/${phase}.md" ]]; then
    echo "${workflow_dir}/phases/${phase}.md"
    return 0
  fi
  
  # 2. Fallback to built-in phases
  if [[ -f "${SCRIPT_DIR}/phases/${phase}.md" ]]; then
    echo "${SCRIPT_DIR}/phases/${phase}.md"
    return 0
  fi
  
  return 1
}
```

**Pros:**
- Standards-based (XDG convention)
- No config needed
- Works out of the box
- Clear priority (project > user > built-in)
- Can extend with custom phases/subagents
- Simple mental model

**Cons:**
- Name collisions possible (project overrides user)
- No explicit enable/disable
- No versioning

**Complexity:** â­â­ (2/5) - Low-Moderate

**This is the RECOMMENDED approach** - good balance of simplicity and power.   â”‚

â”‚ **Approach 7: Hybrid - Convention + Optional Registry**

How it works:
- Uses convention-based discovery (like Approach 6)
- PLUS optional registry for advanced features
- Registry enables metadata, priority, enable/disable

Implementation:
```bash
# Convention-based discovery (always works)
find_workflow_file() {
  local workflow_name=$1
  
  # Check if there's a registry with overrides
  local registry="$HOME/.claude/workflow-registry.yaml"
  
  if [[ -f "$registry" ]]; then
    # Check registry first (explicit configuration)
    local custom_path=$(yq eval ".workflows.\"${workflow_name}\".path" "$registry" 2>/dev/null)
    local enabled=$(yq eval ".workflows.\"${workflow_name}\".enabled // true" "$registry" 2>/dev/null)
    
    if [[ "$enabled" == "false" ]]; then
      # Workflow explicitly disabled
      return 1
    fi
    
    if [[ -n "$custom_path" && "$custom_path" != "null" && -f "$custom_path" ]]; then
      echo "$custom_path"
      return 0
    fi
  fi
  
  # Fall back to convention-based search
  for dir in "./.claude/workflows" "$HOME/.claude/workflows" "${SCRIPT_DIR}/workflows"; do
    if [[ -f "${dir}/${workflow_name}.yaml" ]]; then
      echo "${dir}/${workflow_name}.yaml"
      return 0
    fi
  done
  
  return 1
}
```

Optional registry example:
```yaml
# ~/.claude/workflow-registry.yaml (optional!)
workflows:
  security-audit:
    path: ~/acme-corp/workflows/security-audit.yaml
    enabled: true
    priority: 100
  
  new-feature-plan:
    enabled: false  # Disable built-in, force custom version
  
  custom-review:
    path: /shared/team-workflows/code-review.yaml
    enabled: true
```

**Pros:**
- Works without config (convention)
- Power users can use registry for advanced control
- Gradual adoption (start simple, add registry later)
- Best of both worlds

**Cons:**
- Two systems to maintain
- Potential confusion (which takes precedence?)
- Slightly more complex implementation

â”‚ **Recommendation Summary:**

For use-workflows-v2 skill, I recommend **Approach 6 (XDG Convention)** as the starting point:

**Why Approach 6 is best:**
1. **Simple** - No config files needed, works immediately
2. **Standards-based** - Follows XDG conventions users already know
3. **Intuitive** - Clear hierarchy (project > user > built-in)
4. **Extensible** - Can add features later without breaking changes
5. **Low maintenance** - Minimal code to maintain

**Implementation effort:** ~50-100 lines of code
**Time to implement:** 2-3 hours
**User learning curve:** Minimal (just create ~/.claude/workflows/)

**If more features needed later, can evolve to Approach 7 (Hybrid)**
- Start with convention
- Add optional registry for power users
- Doesn't break existing simple usage

**Avoid:**
- Approach 2 (Symlinks) - Too fragile, confusing
- Approach 5 (Git submodules) - Overkill for this use case
- Approach 4 (Namespaces) - Too verbose for daily use

**Next steps if implementing:**
1. Modify `load_and_validate_workflow()` to check multiple directories
2. Add `list_workflows()` command to show available workflows
3. Update validation tool to check custom locations
4. Add phase/subagent template resolution for custom workflows
5. Update SKILL.md with plugin documentation   â”‚

”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ **Key Implementation Considerations:**

**Question 1: How to handle phase/subagent templates in custom workflows?**

Option A - Templates must be bundled with workflow:
```
~/.claude/workflows/
â”œâ”€â”€ my-custom-workflow.yaml
â”œâ”€â”€ phases/
â”‚   â””â”€â”€ custom-phase.md
â””â”€â”€ subagents/
    â””â”€â”€ registry.yaml
```

Option B - Custom workflows reuse built-in templates (simpler):
```
~/.claude/workflows/
â””â”€â”€ my-custom-workflow.yaml  # References built-in phases
```

**Recommendation:** Support both - fall back to built-in if custom not found

**Question 2: How to handle workflow name conflicts?**

If project has "security-audit" and user has "security-audit":
- Take first match (project wins)
- Show warning about shadowing
- Add `--source` flag to explicitly choose: `--source=user`

**Question 3: Should validation tool check custom locations?**

Yes! Extend validation:
```bash
# Validate all workflows (built-in + custom)
./validate-workflow.sh --all

# Validate specific custom workflow
./validate-workflow.sh ~/.claude/workflows/my-workflow.yaml
```

**Question 4: Backward compatibility?**

All approaches maintain backward compatibility - existing workflows work unchanged.

**Final recommendation:**
Start with **Approach 6** (XDG Convention) because:
- Solves 90% of use cases
- Can evolve to Approach 7 (Hybrid) if needed
- Low risk, high value

â”‚ Starting implementation of Approach 6 (XDG Convention-based plugin system).

**Implementation Plan:**

**Phase 1: Core Discovery (workflow loading)**
- Modify `load_and_validate_workflow()` in `lib/workflow-helpers.sh`
- Check 3 locations: project â†’ user â†’ built-in
- Return workflow file path + source location

**Phase 2: Template Resolution**
- Modify `check_template_override()` to check custom phase templates
- Add custom subagent template resolution
- Fall back to built-in if custom not found

**Phase 3: Tooling**
- Create `list-workflows.sh` to show all available workflows
- Update `validate-workflow.sh` to validate custom workflows
- Handle warnings for name collisions

**Phase 4: Testing & Docs**
- Create test custom workflow
- Verify it loads and runs
- Update SKILL.md with plugin documentation

**What changed:**
- Added `find_workflow_file()` - searches project â†’ user â†’ built-in
- Added `list_available_workflows()` - lists workflows from all locations
- Modified `load_and_validate_workflow()` - uses new discovery

**Phase 2: Custom Phase Template Resolution**

Current `check_template_override()` only checks:
- workflow-specific override: `phases/{workflow}/{phase}.md`
- default: `phases/{phase}.md`

Need to add support for custom phases alongside custom workflows:
- Custom workflow at: `~/.claude/workflows/my-workflow.yaml`
- Custom phases at: `~/.claude/workflows/phases/custom-phase.md`

Implementation strategy:
1. Check if workflow came from custom location ($WORKFLOW_DIR)
2. If custom, look for `${WORKFLOW_DIR}/phases/{phase}.md` first
3. Then fall back to built-in phases

â”‚ Current `check_template_override()` implementation (lines 537-570):

```bash
check_template_override() {
  local workflow=$1
  local phase=$2
  local script_dir=$3

  # Basic traversal safeguards
  if [[ "$workflow" == *".."* || "$phase" == *".."* || "$workflow" == *"/"* || "$phase" == *"/"* ]]; then
    echo "Error: Invalid workflow or phase name" >&2
    return 1
  fi

  # Validate against patterns
  if ! [[ "$workflow" =~ $WORKFLOW_NAME_PATTERN ]] || ! [[ "$phase" =~ $PHASE_NAME_PATTERN ]]; then
    echo "Error: Workflow and phase names must match required patterns" >&2
    return 1
  fi

  local override_path="${script_dir}/$(printf "$PHASE_OVERRIDE_PATTERN" "$workflow" "$phase")"
  if [[ -f "$override_path" ]]; then
    echo "$override_path"
    return 0
  fi

  local default_path="${script_dir}/$(printf "$PHASE_DEFAULT_PATTERN" "$phase")"
  if [[ -f "$default_path" ]]; then
    echo "$default_path"
    return 0
  fi

  echo "Error: Template not found for phase '$phase' (workflow: '$workflow')" >&2
  return 1
}
```

**Modification needed:**
Add check for custom phase templates before checking built-in templates.

Search order:
1. Custom workflow's phases: `${WORKFLOW_DIR}/phases/{phase}.md`
2. Workflow-specific override (built-in): `phases/{workflow}/{phase}.md`
3. Default (built-in): `phases/{phase}.md`

â”‚ Phase template resolution complete! Now custom workflows can bundle their own phase templates.

**What changed:**
- `check_template_override()` now checks custom phase templates first
- Falls back to built-in templates if custom not found
- Uses `$WORKFLOW_DIR` to locate custom phases

**Phase 3: Create Helper Scripts**

Need to create `list-workflows.sh` - a simple script to show all available workflows from all locations.

This will help users discover what workflows are available without errors.

Usage:
```bash
./list-workflows.sh
# Output:
# Available workflows:
#   debugging-plan [built-in]
#   improving-plan [built-in]
#   new-feature-plan [built-in]
#   quick-plan [built-in]
#   refactoring-plan [built-in]
#   my-custom-workflow [user]
#   security-audit [project]
```

**Phase 4: Testing**

I'll create a simple test workflow in ~/.claude/workflows/ to verify:
1. Workflow discovery works
2. Custom workflow loads
3. Can mix custom and built-in phases
4. list-workflows shows it correctly

Test workflow structure:
```

```

The workflow will use:
- `discovery` (built-in phase - should work)
- `custom-test-phase` (custom phase - should work)

This tests both custom and built-in phase resolution.   â”‚

â”‚ Found the bug! The environment variables set in `load_and_validate_workflow` don't persist because:

1. `initialize_workflow_context` is called in a subshell: `context_json=$(initialize_workflow_context ...)`
2. Environment variables exported inside that subshell are lost when it exits
3. Later calls to `check_template_override` don't see `$WORKFLOW_DIR`

**Solution:** Include workflow directory info in the JSON output of `initialize_workflow_context`, then let scripts extract and set those env vars.

I'll modify `initialize_workflow_context` to add:
- `workflow_dir`
- `workflow_source_type`  
- `workflow_file_path`

To its JSON output.

Then update scripts to set these as environment variables after initializing context.   â”‚

â”‚ Found the root cause! The problem is that `export` statements inside a function don't propagate to the calling scope in the same way I expected.

When `initialize_workflow_context` calls `load_and_validate_workflow`, the exports happen but then when we try to read `${WORKFLOW_DIR:-}` later in the same function, it's empty.

**Better solution:**
Modify `load_and_validate_workflow` to return a JSON object containing BOTH the workflow JSON and the metadata (workflow_dir, source_type, file_path). Then `initialize_workflow_context` can extract these and include them in its output.

Structure:
```json
{
  "workflow_json": {...},
  "workflow_dir": "/path/to/workflows",
  "workflow_source_type": "user",
  "workflow_file_path": "/path/to/workflows/test-plugin.yaml"
}
```

This way all the info flows through return values, not environment variables.   â”‚
